class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><span class="fn">Evelyn Waugh</span></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Evelynwaugh.jpeg" Waugh</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Arthur Evelyn St. John Waugh</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90r/: &#39;ar&#39; in &#39;far&#39;">\xc9\x91\xcb\x90r</span><span title="/\xce\xb8/: &#39;th&#39; in &#39;thigh&#39;">\xce\xb8</span><span title="/\xc9\x99r/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">\xc9\x99r</span></span> <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="/i\xcb\x90/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">i\xcb\x90</span><span title="&#39;v&#39; in &#39;vie&#39;">v</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span> <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/d\xca\x92/: &#39;j&#39; in &#39;jam&#39;">d\xca\x92</span><span title="/\xc9\x99n/: &#39;on&#39; in &#39;button&#39;">\xc9\x99n</span></span> <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90/: &#39;au&#39; in &#39;fraud&#39;">\xc9\x94\xcb\x90</span></span>/</a></span></span>; 28 October 1903&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 10 April 1966) was an English writer of novels, biographies, and travel books, and he was also a prolific journalist and book reviewer. His most famous works include the early satires <i><a href="/wiki/Decline_and_Fall" title="Decline and Fall">Decline and Fall</a></i> (1928) and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Handful_of_Dust" title="A Handful of Dust">A Handful of Dust</a></i> (1934), the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Brideshead_Revisited" title="Brideshead Revisited">Brideshead Revisited</a></i> (1945), and the Second World War trilogy <i><a href="/wiki/Sword_of_Honour" title="Sword of Honour">Sword of Honour</a></i> (1952\xe2\x80\x9361). He is recognised as one of the great prose stylists of the English language in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh was the son of a publisher, educated at <a href="/wiki/Lancing_College" title="Lancing College">Lancing College</a> and then at <a href="/wiki/Hertford_College,_Oxford" title="Hertford College, Oxford">Hertford College, Oxford</a>. He worked briefly as a schoolmaster before he became a full-time writer. As a young man, he acquired many fashionable and aristocratic friends and developed a taste for <a href="/wiki/Country_house" class="mw-redirect" title="Country house">country house</a> society. He travelled extensively in the 1930s, often as a special newspaper correspondent; he reported from <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Abyssinia</a> at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Ethiopian_War" title="Second Italo-Ethiopian War">1935 Italian invasion</a>. He served in the British armed forces throughout the Second World War, first in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines" title="Royal Marines">Royal Marines</a> and then in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horse_Guards" title="Royal Horse Guards">Royal Horse Guards</a>. He was a perceptive writer who used the experiences and the wide range of people whom he encountered in his works of fiction, generally to humorous effect. Waugh\'s detachment was such that he fictionalised his own mental breakdown which occurred in the early 1950s.\n</p><p>Waugh converted to <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> in 1930 after his first marriage failed. His traditionalist stance led him to strongly oppose all attempts to reform the Church, and the changes by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a> (1962\xe2\x80\x9365) greatly disturbed his sensibilities, especially the introduction of the vernacular <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_the_Catholic_Church" title="Mass in the Catholic Church">Mass</a>. That blow to his religious traditionalism, his dislike for the <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> culture of the postwar world, and the decline of his health all darkened his final years, but he continued to write. He displayed to the world a mask of indifference, but he was capable of great kindness to those whom he considered his friends. After his death in 1966 he acquired a following of new readers through the film and television versions of his works, such as the television serial <i><a href="/wiki/Brideshead_Revisited_(TV_serial)" title="Brideshead Revisited (TV serial)">Brideshead Revisited</a></i> (1981).\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_background">Family background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Family background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:157px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Cockburn,_Lord_Cockburn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/Henry_Cockburn%2C_Lord_Cockburn.jpg" width="155" height="211" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="155" data-file-height="211" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Henry_Cockburn,_Lord_Cockburn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Cockburn,_Lord_Cockburn" title="Henry Cockburn, Lord Cockburn">Lord Cockburn</a>, the Scottish judge, was one of Waugh\'s great-great-grandfathers.</div></div></div>\n<p>Arthur Evelyn St. John Waugh was born on 28 October 1903 to <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Waugh" title="Arthur Waugh">Arthur Waugh</a> (1866\xe2\x80\x931943) and Catherine Charlotte Raban (1870\xe2\x80\x931954), into a family with English, Scottish, Welsh, Irish and <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> origins. Distinguished forebears include <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cockburn,_Lord_Cockburn" title="Henry Cockburn, Lord Cockburn">Lord Cockburn</a> (1779\xe2\x80\x931854), a leading Scottish advocate and judge, <a href="/wiki/William_Morgan_(actuary)" title="William Morgan (actuary)">William Morgan</a> (1750\xe2\x80\x931833), a pioneer of <a href="/wiki/Actuarial_science" title="Actuarial science">actuarial science</a> who served <a href="/wiki/The_Equitable_Life_Assurance_Society" title="The Equitable Life Assurance Society">the Equitable Life Assurance Society</a> for 56 years, and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Henry_Gosse" title="Philip Henry Gosse">Philip Henry Gosse</a> (1810\xe2\x80\x931888), a natural scientist who became notorious through his depiction as a religious fanatic in his son <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Gosse" title="Edmund Gosse">Edmund\'s</a> memoir <i><a href="/wiki/Father_and_Son_(book)" title="Father and Son (book)">Father and Son</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Among ancestors bearing the Waugh name, the Rev. Alexander Waugh (1754\xe2\x80\x931827) was a minister in the <a href="/wiki/United_Secession_Church" title="United Secession Church">Secession Church of Scotland</a> who helped found the <a href="/wiki/London_Missionary_Society" title="London Missionary Society">London Missionary Society</a> and was one of the leading <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">Nonconformist</a> preachers of his day.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> His grandson Alexander Waugh (1840\xe2\x80\x931906) was a country medical practitioner, who bullied his wife and children and became known in the Waugh family as "the Brute". The elder of his two sons, born in 1866, was <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Waugh" title="Arthur Waugh">Arthur Waugh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After attending <a href="/wiki/Sherborne_School" title="Sherborne School">Sherborne School</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_College,_Oxford" title="New College, Oxford">New College, Oxford</a>, Arthur Waugh began a career in publishing and as a <a href="/wiki/Literary_critic" class="mw-redirect" title="Literary critic">literary critic</a>.  In 1902 he became managing director of <a href="/wiki/Chapman_and_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Chapman and Hall">Chapman and Hall</a>, publishers of the works of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> He had married Catherine Raban (1870\xe2\x80\x931954)<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> in 1893; their first son <a href="/wiki/Alec_Waugh" title="Alec Waugh">Alexander Raban Waugh</a> (always known as Alec) was born on 8 July 1898. Alec Waugh later became a novelist of note.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> At the time of his birth the family were living in <a href="/wiki/North_London" title="North London">North London</a>, at Hillfield Road, <a href="/wiki/West_Hampstead" title="West Hampstead">West Hampstead</a> where, on 28 October 1903, the couple\'s second son was born, "in great haste before Dr Andrews could arrive", Catherine recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> On 7 January 1904 the boy was christened Arthur Evelyn St John Waugh but was known in the family and in the wider world as Evelyn.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;n 1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Childhood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golders_Green_and_Heath_Mount">Golders Green and Heath Mount</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Golders Green and Heath Mount">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1907, the Waugh family left Hillfield Road for Underhill, a house which Arthur had built in North End Road, <a href="/wiki/Hampstead" title="Hampstead">Hampstead</a>, close to <a href="/wiki/Golders_Green" title="Golders Green">Golders Green</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> then a semi-rural area of dairy farms, market gardens and bluebell woods.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Evelyn received his first school lessons at home, from his mother, with whom he formed a particularly close relationship; his father, Arthur Waugh, was a more distant figure, whose close bond with his elder son, Alec, was such that Evelyn often felt excluded.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In September 1910, Evelyn began as a day pupil at <a href="/wiki/Heath_Mount_School" title="Heath Mount School">Heath Mount</a> preparatory school. By then, he was a lively boy of many interests, who already had written and completed "The Curse of the Horse Race", his first story.<sup id="cite_ref-S40_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S40-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Waugh spent six relatively contented years at Heath Mount; on his own assertion he was "quite a clever little boy", who was seldom distressed or overawed by his lessons.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Physically pugnacious, Evelyn was inclined to bully weaker boys; among his victims was the future society photographer <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Beaton" title="Cecil Beaton">Cecil Beaton</a>, who never forgot the experience.<sup id="cite_ref-S40_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S40-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Outside school, he and other neighbourhood children performed plays, usually written by Waugh.<sup id="cite_ref-H30_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H30-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> On the basis of the xenophobia fostered by the genre books of <a href="/wiki/Invasion_literature" title="Invasion literature">Invasion literature</a>, that the Germans were about to invade Britain, Waugh organised his friends into the "Pistol Troop", who built a fort, went on man\xc5\x93uvres and paraded in makeshift uniforms.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In 1914, after the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> began, Waugh and other boys from the Boy Scout Troop of Heath Mount School were sometimes employed as messengers at the <a href="/wiki/War_Office" title="War Office">War Office</a>; Evelyn loitered about the War Office in hope of glimpsing <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener" class="mw-redirect" title="Horatio Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener">Lord Kitchener</a>, but never did.<sup id="cite_ref-Stannard42_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stannard42-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Family holidays usually were spent with the Waugh aunts, at <a href="/wiki/Midsomer_Norton" title="Midsomer Norton">Midsomer Norton</a>, in a house lit with oil lamps, a time that Waugh recalled with delight, many years later.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> At Midsomer Norton, Evelyn became deeply interested in <a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">high Anglican</a> church rituals, the initial stirrings of the spiritual dimension that later dominated his perspective of life, and he served as an <a href="/wiki/Altar_boy" class="mw-redirect" title="Altar boy">altar boy</a> at the local Anglican church.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> During his last year at Heath Mount, Waugh established and edited <i>The Cynic</i> school magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-S40_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S40-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lancing">Lancing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lancing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lancing_College_Chapel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lancing_College_Chapel.jpg/220px-Lancing_College_Chapel.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lancing_College_Chapel.jpg/330px-Lancing_College_Chapel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Lancing_College_Chapel.jpg/440px-Lancing_College_Chapel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="447" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lancing_College_Chapel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lancing_College" title="Lancing College">Lancing College</a> Chapel</div></div></div>\n<p>Like his father before him, Alec Waugh went to school at Sherborne, and, it was presumed by the family that Evelyn would follow, but in 1915, the school asked Evelyn\'s older brother Alec to leave, after a <a href="/wiki/Homosexual" class="mw-redirect" title="Homosexual">homosexual</a> relationship came to light. Alec departed Sherborne for military training as an <a href="/wiki/Military_officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Military officer">officer</a>, and, while awaiting confirmation of his <a href="/wiki/Commission_(document)" title="Commission (document)">commission</a>, wrote <i>The Loom of Youth</i> (1917), a novel of school life, which alluded to homosexual friendships at a school that was recognisably Sherborne. The public sensation caused by Alec\'s novel so offended the school that it became impossible for Evelyn to go there. In May 1917, much to his annoyance, he was sent to <a href="/wiki/Lancing_College" title="Lancing College">Lancing College</a>, in his opinion, a decidedly inferior school.<sup id="cite_ref-Stannard42_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stannard42-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh soon overcame his initial aversion to Lancing, settled in and established his reputation as an <a href="/wiki/Aesthete" class="mw-redirect" title="Aesthete">aesthete</a>.  In November 1917 his essay "In Defence of Cubism" (1917) was accepted by and published in the arts magazine <i>Drawing and Design</i>; it was his first published article.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Within the school, he became mildly subversive, mocking the school\'s cadet corps and founding the Corpse Club "for those who were bored stiff".<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The end of the war saw the return to the school of younger masters such as <a href="/wiki/J._F._Roxburgh" title="J. F. Roxburgh">J. F. Roxburgh</a>, who encouraged Waugh to write and predicted a great future for him.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;n 3&#93;</a></sup>  Another mentor, Francis Crease, taught Waugh the arts of <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a> and decorative design; some of the boy\'s work was good enough to be used by Chapman and Hall on book jackets.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his later years at Lancing, Waugh achieved success as a house captain, editor of the school magazine and president of the <a href="/wiki/Debating_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Debating society">debating society</a>, and won numerous art and literature prizes.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> He also shed most of his religious beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> He started a novel of school life, untitled, but abandoned the effort after writing around 5,000 words.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> He ended his schooldays by winning a scholarship to read Modern History at <a href="/wiki/Hertford_College,_Oxford" title="Hertford College, Oxford">Hertford College, Oxford</a>, and left Lancing in December 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxford">Oxford</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Oxford">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hertford_college_Old_Quadrangle_under_the_snow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hertford_college_Old_Quadrangle_under_the_snow.jpg/220px-Hertford_college_Old_Quadrangle_under_the_snow.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hertford_college_Old_Quadrangle_under_the_snow.jpg/330px-Hertford_college_Old_Quadrangle_under_the_snow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hertford_college_Old_Quadrangle_under_the_snow.jpg/440px-Hertford_college_Old_Quadrangle_under_the_snow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hertford_college_Old_Quadrangle_under_the_snow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hertford_College,_Oxford" title="Hertford College, Oxford">Hertford College, Oxford</a>; Old Quadrangle</div></div></div>\n<p>Waugh arrived in Oxford in January 1922. He was soon writing to old friends at Lancing about the pleasures of his new life; he informed <a href="/wiki/Tom_Driberg" title="Tom Driberg">Tom Driberg</a>: "I do no work here and never go to Chapel".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> During his first two terms, he generally followed convention; he smoked a pipe, bought a bicycle, and gave his maiden speech at the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Union" title="Oxford Union">Oxford Union</a>, opposing the motion that "This House would welcome Prohibition".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Waugh wrote reports on Union debates for both Oxford magazines, <i><a href="/wiki/Cherwell_(newspaper)" title="Cherwell (newspaper)">Cherwell</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Isis_magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Isis magazine">Isis</a></i>, and he acted as a film critic for <i>Isis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> He also became secretary of the Hertford College debating society, "an onerous but not honorific post", he told Driberg.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Although Waugh tended to regard his scholarship as a reward for past efforts rather than a stepping-stone to future academic success, he did sufficient work in his first two terms to pass his "History Previous", an essential preliminary examination.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The arrival in Oxford in October 1922 of the sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Old_Etonians" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Etonians">Etonians</a> <a href="/wiki/Harold_Acton" title="Harold Acton">Harold Acton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brian_Howard_(poet)" title="Brian Howard (poet)">Brian Howard</a> changed Waugh\'s Oxford life. Acton and Howard rapidly became the centre of an <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> circle known as the <a href="/wiki/Hypocrites%27_Club" title="Hypocrites&#39; Club">Hypocrites\' Club</a> (Waugh was the secretary of the club),<sup id="cite_ref-Lebedoff_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lebedoff-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> whose artistic, social and homosexual values Waugh adopted enthusiastically;<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> he later wrote: "It was the stamping ground of half my Oxford life".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> He began drinking heavily, and embarked on the first of several homosexual relationships, the most lasting of which were with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Pares" title="Richard Pares">Richard Pares</a> and Alastair Graham.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> He continued to write reviews and short stories for the university journals, and developed a reputation as a talented graphic artist, but formal study largely ceased.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This neglect led to a bitter feud between Waugh and his history tutor, <a href="/wiki/C._R._M._F._Cruttwell" title="C. R. M. F. Cruttwell">C. R. M. F. Cruttwell</a>, dean (and later principal) of Hertford College. When Cruttwell advised him to mend his ways, Waugh responded in a manner which, he admitted later, was "fatuously haughty",<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> from then on, relations between the two descended into mutual hatred.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Waugh continued the feud long after his Oxford days by using Cruttwell\'s name in his early novels for a succession of ludicrous, ignominious or odious minor characters.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh\'s dissipated lifestyle continued into his final Oxford year, 1924. A letter written that year to a Lancing friend, <a href="/wiki/Dudley_Carew" title="Dudley Carew">Dudley Carew</a>, hints at severe emotional pressures: "I have been living very intensely these last three weeks. For the last fortnight I have been nearly insane.... I may perhaps one day in a later time tell you some of the things that have happened".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> He did just enough work to pass his final examinations in the summer of 1924 with a third-class. However, as he had begun at Hertford in the second term of the 1921\xe2\x80\x9322 academic year, Waugh had completed only eight terms\' residence when he sat his finals, rather than the nine required under the university\'s statutes. His poor results led to the loss of his scholarship, which made it impossible for him to return to Oxford for that final term, so he left without his degree.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Back at home,  Waugh began a novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Temple_at_Thatch" title="The Temple at Thatch">The Temple at Thatch</a></i>, and worked with some of his fellow Hypocrites on a film, <i>The Scarlet Woman</i>, which was shot partly in the gardens at Underhill. He spent much of the rest of the summer in the company of Alastair Graham; after Graham departed for <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, Waugh enrolled for the autumn at a London art school, <a href="/wiki/Heatherley_School_of_Fine_Art" title="Heatherley School of Fine Art">Heatherley\'s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-S93_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S93-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career">Early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schoolmaster_and_incipient_writer">Schoolmaster and incipient writer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Schoolmaster and incipient writer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti_001.jpg/170px-Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti_001.jpg" width="170" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti_001.jpg/255px-Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti_001.jpg/340px-Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="669" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti" title="Dante Gabriel Rossetti">Dante Gabriel Rossetti</a>, the subject of Waugh\'s first full-length book (1927)</div></div></div>\n<p>Waugh began at Heatherley\'s in late September 1924, but became bored with the routine and quickly abandoned his course.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> He spent weeks partying in London and Oxford before the overriding need for money led him to apply through an agency for a teaching job. Almost at once, he secured a post at Arnold House, a boys\' preparatory school in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>, beginning in January 1925. He took with him the notes for his novel, <i>The Temple at Thatch</i>, intending to work on it in his spare time. Despite the gloomy ambience of the school, Waugh did his best to fulfil the requirements of his position, but a brief return to London and Oxford during the Easter holiday only exacerbated his sense of isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the summer of 1925, Waugh\'s outlook briefly improved, with the prospect of a job in <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a>, Italy, as secretary to the Scottish writer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kenneth_Scott_Moncrieff" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Kenneth Scott Moncrieff">Charles Kenneth Scott Moncrieff</a>, who was engaged on the English translations of <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Proust" title="Marcel Proust">Marcel Proust</a>\'s works. Believing that the job was his, Waugh resigned his position at Arnold House. He had meantime sent the early chapters of his novel to Acton for assessment and criticism. Acton\'s reply was so coolly dismissive that Waugh immediately burnt his manuscript; shortly afterwards, before he left <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>, he learned that the Moncrieff job had fallen through.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The twin blows were sufficient for him to consider suicide. He records that he went down to a nearby beach and, leaving a note with his clothes, walked out to sea. An attack by jellyfish changed his mind, and he returned quickly to the shore.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the following two years Waugh taught at schools in <a href="/wiki/Aston_Clinton" title="Aston Clinton">Aston Clinton</a> (from which he was dismissed for the attempted drunken seduction of a school matron) and <a href="/wiki/Notting_Hill" title="Notting Hill">Notting Hill</a> in London.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> He considered alternative careers in printing or cabinet-making, and attended evening classes in carpentry at Holborn Polytechnic while continuing to write.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> A short story, "The Balance", written in an experimental <a href="/wiki/Modernist_literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Modernist literature">modernist</a> style, became his first commercially published fiction, when it was included by Chapman and Hall in a 1926 anthology, <i>Georgian Stories</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> An extended essay on the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Raphaelite_Brotherhood" title="Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood">Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood</a> was printed privately by Alastair Graham, using by agreement the press of the <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare_Head_Press" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare Head Press">Shakespeare Head Press</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stratford-upon-Avon" title="Stratford-upon-Avon">Stratford-upon-Avon</a>, where he was undergoing training as a printer.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> This led to a contract from the publishers <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Duckworth_and_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerald Duckworth and Company">Duckworths</a> for a full-length biography of <a href="/wiki/Dante_Gabriel_Rossetti" title="Dante Gabriel Rossetti">Dante Gabriel Rossetti</a>, which Waugh wrote during 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-Sykes73_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sykes73-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> He also began working on a <a href="/wiki/Comic_novel" title="Comic novel">comic novel</a>; after several temporary working titles this became <i><a href="/wiki/Decline_and_Fall" title="Decline and Fall">Decline and Fall</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hastings168_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings168-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Having given up teaching, he had no regular employment except for a short, unsuccessful stint as a reporter on the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">Daily Express</a></i> in April\xe2\x80\x93May 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> That year he met (possibly through his brother <a href="/wiki/Alec_Waugh" title="Alec Waugh">Alec</a>) and fell in love with <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Gardner" title="Evelyn Gardner">Evelyn Gardner</a>, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Gardner,_1st_Baron_Burghclere" title="Herbert Gardner, 1st Baron Burghclere">Lord and Lady Burghclere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id=".22He-Evelyn.22_and_.22She-Evelyn.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;He-Evelyn&quot;_and_&quot;She-Evelyn&quot;">"He-Evelyn" and "She-Evelyn"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: &quot;He-Evelyn&quot; and &quot;She-Evelyn&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:069_Canonbury_Square.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/069_Canonbury_Square.jpg/170px-069_Canonbury_Square.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/069_Canonbury_Square.jpg/255px-069_Canonbury_Square.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/069_Canonbury_Square.jpg/340px-069_Canonbury_Square.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="2007" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:069_Canonbury_Square.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Canonbury Square, where Waugh and Evelyn Gardner lived during their brief marriage</div></div></div>\n<p>In December 1927, Waugh and Evelyn Gardner became engaged, despite the opposition of Lady Burghclere, who felt that Waugh lacked moral fibre and kept unsuitable company.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Among their friends, they quickly became known as "He-Evelyn" and "She-Evelyn".<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Waugh was at this time dependent on a \xc2\xa34-a-week allowance from his father and the small sums he could earn from book reviewing and journalism.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The Rossetti biography was published to a generally favourable reception in April 1928: <a href="/wiki/J._C._Squire" title="J. C. Squire">J. C. Squire</a> in <i>The Observer</i> praised the book\'s elegance and wit; Acton gave cautious approval; and the novelist <a href="/wiki/Rebecca_West" title="Rebecca West">Rebecca West</a> wrote to express how much she had enjoyed the book. Less pleasing to Waugh was the <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Literary_Supplement" class="mw-redirect" title="Times Literary Supplement">Times Literary Supplement</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s references to him as "Miss Waugh".<sup id="cite_ref-Hastings168_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings168-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When <i>Decline and Fall</i> was completed, Duckworths objected to its "obscenity", but Chapman and Hall agreed to publish it.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> This was sufficient for Waugh and Gardner to bring forward their wedding plans. They were married in St Paul\'s Church, Portman Square, on 27 June 1928, with only Acton, Alec Waugh and the bride\'s friend <a href="/wiki/Lady_Pansy_Lamb" title="Lady Pansy Lamb">Pansy Pakenham</a> present.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The couple made their home in a small flat in Canonbury Square, <a href="/wiki/Islington" title="Islington">Islington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The first months of the marriage were overshadowed by a lack of money, and by Gardner\'s poor health, which persisted into the autumn.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 1928, <i>Decline and Fall</i> was published to almost unanimous praise. By December, the book was into its third printing, and the American publishing rights were sold for $500.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In the afterglow of his success, Waugh was commissioned to write travel articles in return for a free <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> cruise, which he and Gardner began in February 1929, as an extended, delayed honeymoon. The trip was disrupted when Gardner contracted <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> and was carried ashore to the British hospital in <a href="/wiki/Port_Said" title="Port Said">Port Said</a>. The couple returned home in June, after her recovery. A month later, without warning, Gardner confessed that their mutual friend, <a href="/wiki/John_Heygate" title="John Heygate">John Heygate</a>, had become her lover. After an attempted reconciliation failed, a shocked and dismayed Waugh filed for divorce on 3 September 1929. The couple apparently met again only once, during the process for the <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulment</a> of their marriage a few years later.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Years_of_celebrity">Years of celebrity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Years of celebrity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition">Recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Waugh\'s first biographer, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Sykes_(author)" title="Christopher Sykes (author)">Christopher Sykes</a>, records that after the divorce friends "saw, or believed they saw, a new hardness and bitterness" in Waugh\'s outlook.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, despite a letter to Acton in which he wrote that he "did not know it was possible to be so miserable and live",<sup id="cite_ref-Amory39_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amory39-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> he soon resumed his professional and social life. He finished his second novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Vile_Bodies" title="Vile Bodies">Vile Bodies</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Patey33_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey33-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and wrote articles including (ironically, he thought) one for the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i> on the meaning of the marriage ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-Amory39_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amory39-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> During this period Waugh began the practice of staying at the various houses of his friends; he was to have no settled home for the next eight years.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey33_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey33-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Vile Bodies</i>, a satire on the <a href="/wiki/Bright_Young_People" class="mw-redirect" title="Bright Young People">Bright Young People</a> of the 1920s, was published on 19 January 1930 and was Waugh\'s first major commercial success. Despite its quasi-biblical title, the book is dark, bitter, "a manifesto of disillusionment", according to biographer Martin Stannard.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> As a best-selling author Waugh could now command larger fees for his journalism.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey33_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey33-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Amid regular work for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graphic" title="The Graphic">The Graphic</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Town_and_Country_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Town and Country (magazine)">Town and Country</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Bazaar" title="Harper&#39;s Bazaar">Harper\'s Bazaar</a></i>, he quickly wrote <i>Labels</i>, a detached account of his honeymoon cruise with She-Evelyn.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey33_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey33-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_to_Catholicism">Conversion to Catholicism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Conversion to Catholicism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 29 September 1930, Waugh was received into the Catholic Church. This shocked his family and surprised some of his friends, but he had contemplated the step for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey35_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey35-78">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> He had lost his Anglicanism at Lancing and had led an irreligious life at Oxford, but there are references in his diaries from the mid-1920s to religious discussion and regular churchgoing. On 22 December 1925, Waugh wrote: "Claud and I took Audrey to supper and sat up until 7 in the morning arguing about the Roman Church".<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The entry for 20 February 1927 includes, "I am to visit a Father Underhill about being a parson".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the period, Waugh was influenced by his friend Olivia Plunket-Greene, who had converted in 1925 and of whom Waugh later wrote, "She bullied me into the Church".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> It was she who led him to Father <a href="/wiki/Martin_D%27Arcy" title="Martin D&#39;Arcy">Martin D\'Arcy</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a>, who persuaded Waugh "on firm intellectual convictions but little emotion" that "the Christian revelation was genuine". In 1949, Waugh explained that his conversion followed his realisation that life was "unintelligible and unendurable without God".<sup id="cite_ref-Gallagher366_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher366-82">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writer_and_traveller">Writer and traveller</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Writer and traveller">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CropSelassie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/CropSelassie.jpg/170px-CropSelassie.jpg" width="170" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/CropSelassie.jpg/255px-CropSelassie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/CropSelassie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="341" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CropSelassie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie" title="Haile Selassie">Haile Selassie</a>, whose coronation Waugh attended in 1930 on the first of his three trips to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Abyssinia</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On 10 October 1930, Waugh, representing several newspapers, departed for <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Abyssinia</a> to cover the coronation of <a href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie" title="Haile Selassie">Haile Selassie</a>. He reported the event as "an elaborate propaganda effort" to convince the world that Abyssinia was a civilised nation that concealed that the emperor had achieved power through barbarous means.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> A subsequent journey through the <a href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="British East Africa">British East Africa</a> colonies and the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">Belgian Congo</a> formed the basis of two books; the travelogue <i>Remote People</i> (1931) and the comic novel <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Mischief" title="Black Mischief">Black Mischief</a></i> (1932).<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Waugh\'s next extended trip, in the winter of 1932\xe2\x80\x931933, was to <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">British Guiana</a> (now Guyana) in South America, possibly taken to distract him from a long and unrequited passion for the socialite <a href="/wiki/Teresa_Jungman" title="Teresa Jungman">Teresa Jungman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> On arrival in <a href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Guyana" title="Georgetown, Guyana">Georgetown</a>, Waugh arranged a river trip by steam launch into the interior. He travelled on via several staging-posts to <a href="/wiki/Boa_Vista,_Roraima" title="Boa Vista, Roraima">Boa Vista</a> in Brazil, and then took a convoluted overland journey back to Georgetown.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> His various adventures and encounters found their way into two further books: his travel account <i>Ninety-two Days</i>, and the novel <i><a href="/wiki/A_Handful_of_Dust" title="A Handful of Dust">A Handful of Dust</a></i>, both published in 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Back from South America, Waugh faced accusations of obscenity and <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">blasphemy</a> from the Catholic journal <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tablet" title="The Tablet">The Tablet</a></i>, which objected to passages in <i>Black Mischief</i>. He defended himself in an open letter to the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Westminster" title="Archbishop of Westminster">Archbishop of Westminster</a>, Cardinal <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bourne" title="Francis Bourne">Francis Bourne</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> which remained unpublished until 1980. In the summer of 1934, he went on an expedition to <a href="/wiki/Spitsbergen" title="Spitsbergen">Spitsbergen</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, an experience he did not enjoy and of which he made minimal literary use.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> On his return, determined to write a major Catholic biography, he selected the <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> martyr <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Campion" title="Edmund Campion">Edmund Campion</a> as his subject. The book, published in 1935, caused controversy by its forthright pro-Catholic, anti-<a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> stance but brought its writer the <a href="/wiki/Hawthornden_Prize" title="Hawthornden Prize">Hawthornden Prize</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> He returned to Abyssinia in August 1935 to report the opening stages of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">Second Italo-Abyssinian War</a> for the <i>Daily Mail</i>. Waugh, on the basis of his earlier visit, considered Abyssinia  "a savage place which <a href="/wiki/Mussolini" class="mw-redirect" title="Mussolini">Mussolini</a> was doing well to tame" according to his fellow reporter, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Deedes" title="Bill Deedes">William Deedes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Waugh saw little action and was not wholly serious in his role as a war correspondent.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Deedes remarks on the older writer\'s snobbery: "None of us quite measured up to the company he liked to keep back at home".<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> However, in the face of imminent Italian air attacks, Deedes found Waugh\'s courage "deeply reassuring".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Waugh wrote up his Abyssinian experiences in a book, <i>Waugh in Abyssinia</i> (1936), which <a href="/wiki/Rose_Macaulay" title="Rose Macaulay">Rose Macaulay</a> dismissed as a "fascist tract" on account of its pro-Italian tone.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> A better-known account is his novel <i><a href="/wiki/Scoop_(novel)" title="Scoop (novel)">Scoop</a></i> (1938) in which the protagonist, William Boot, is loosely based on Deedes.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Among Waugh\'s growing circle of friends were <a href="/wiki/Diana_Mitford" title="Diana Mitford">Diana Guinness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Guinness,_2nd_Baron_Moyne" title="Bryan Guinness, 2nd Baron Moyne">Bryan Guinness</a> (dedicatees of <i><a href="/wiki/Vile_Bodies" title="Vile Bodies">Vile Bodies</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Lady_Diana_Cooper" title="Lady Diana Cooper">Lady Diana Cooper</a> and her husband <a href="/wiki/Duff_Cooper" title="Duff Cooper">Duff Cooper</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Mitford" title="Nancy Mitford">Nancy Mitford</a> who was originally a friend of Evelyn Gardner\'s,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Earl_Beauchamp" title="Earl Beauchamp">Lygon sisters</a>. Waugh had known <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Patrick_Lygon" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Patrick Lygon">Hugh Patrick Lygon</a> at Oxford; now he was introduced to the girls and their country house, <a href="/wiki/Madresfield_Court" title="Madresfield Court">Madresfield Court</a>, which became the closest that he had to a home during his years of wandering.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In 1933, on a Greek islands cruise, he was introduced by Father D\'Arcy to Gabriel Herbert, eldest daughter of the late explorer <a href="/wiki/Aubrey_Herbert" title="Aubrey Herbert">Aubrey Herbert</a>. When the cruise ended Waugh was invited to stay at the Herbert family\'s villa in <a href="/wiki/Portofino" title="Portofino">Portofino</a>, where he first met Gabriel\'s 17-year-old sister, Laura.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_marriage">Second marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Second marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On his conversion, Waugh had accepted that he would be unable to remarry while Evelyn Gardner was alive. However, he wanted a wife and children, and in October 1933, he began proceedings for the <a href="/wiki/Annulment_(Catholic_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Annulment (Catholic Church)">annulment</a> of the marriage on the grounds of "lack of real consent". The case was heard by an <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_court" title="Ecclesiastical court">ecclesiastical tribunal</a> in London, but a delay in the submission of the papers to Rome meant that the annulment was not granted until 4 July 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In the meantime, following their initial encounter in Portofino, Waugh had fallen in love with Laura Herbert<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> a daughter of Hon. <a href="/wiki/Aubrey_Herbert" title="Aubrey Herbert">Aubrey Herbert</a> (1880-1923), of <a href="/wiki/Pixton_Park" title="Pixton Park">Pixton Park</a> in Somerset (a younger son of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Herbert,_4th_Earl_of_Carnarvon" title="Henry Herbert, 4th Earl of Carnarvon">Henry Herbert, 4th Earl of Carnarvon</a> (1831-1890)) who had inherited his father\'s grand residence built by him in 1874 at Portofino named <i>Villa Alta Chiara</i>, an Italian rendering of "<a href="/wiki/Highclere_Castle" title="Highclere Castle">Highclere</a>",  the English seat of the Earldom.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> He proposed marriage, by letter, in spring 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> There were initial misgivings from the <a href="/wiki/Herbert_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbert family">Herberts</a>, an aristocratic Catholic family; as a further complication, Laura Herbert was a cousin of Evelyn Gardner.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Despite opposition from <a href="/wiki/Auberon_Herbert_(landowner)" title="Auberon Herbert (landowner)">Auberon Herbert</a> (1922-1974), Laura\'s brother, the marriage took place on 17 April 1937 at the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_our_Lady_of_the_Assumption_and_St_Gregory,_Westminster" class="mw-redirect" title="Church of our Lady of the Assumption and St Gregory, Westminster">Church of the Assumption</a> in Warwick Street, London.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Waugh never forgave Auberon\'s opposition, and an unflattering portrayal of a brother-in-law, \'Boy\' Mulcaster, featuring some of his characteristics appears in Waugh\'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Brideshead_Revisited" title="Brideshead Revisited">Brideshead Revisited</a></i>. As a wedding present the bride\'s grandmother bought the couple Piers Court, a country house near <a href="/wiki/Stinchcombe,_Gloucestershire" class="mw-redirect" title="Stinchcombe, Gloucestershire">Stinchcombe</a> in Gloucestershire.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The couple had seven children, one of whom died in infancy. Their first child, a daughter, Maria Teresa, was born on 9 March 1938 and a son, <a href="/wiki/Auberon_Waugh" title="Auberon Waugh">Auberon Alexander Waugh</a>, on 17 November 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Between these events, <i>Scoop</i> was published in May 1938 to wide critical acclaim.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In August 1938 Waugh, with Laura, made a three-month trip to <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> after which he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Robbery_Under_Law" title="Robbery Under Law">Robbery Under Law</a></i>, based on his experiences there. In the book he spelled out clearly his conservative credo; he later described the book as dealing "little with travel and much with political questions".<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_Marine_and_commando">Royal Marine and commando</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Royal Marine and commando">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Waugh left Piers Court on 1 September 1939, at the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> and moved his young family to <a href="/wiki/Pixton_Park" title="Pixton Park">Pixton Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a>, the Herbert family\'s country seat, while he sought military employment.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> He also began writing a novel in a new style, using first-person narration<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> but abandoned work on it when he was commissioned into the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Marines" title="Royal Marines">Royal Marines</a> in December and entered training at <a href="/wiki/Chatham,_Kent" title="Chatham, Kent">Chatham</a> naval base.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> He never completed the novel: fragments were eventually published as <i>Work Suspended and Other Stories</i> (1943).<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh\'s daily training routine left him with "so stiff a spine that he found it painful even to pick up a pen".<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In April 1940, he was temporarily promoted to <a href="/wiki/Captain_(British_Army_and_Royal_Marines)" title="Captain (British Army and Royal Marines)">captain</a> and given command of a <a href="/wiki/Company_(military_unit)" title="Company (military unit)">company</a> of marines, but he proved an unpopular officer, being haughty and curt with his men.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Even after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">German invasion of the Low Countries</a> (10 May\xe2\x80\x9322 June 1940), his battalion was not called into action.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Waugh\'s inability to adapt to regimental life meant that he soon lost his command, and he became the battalion\'s Intelligence Officer. In that role, he finally saw action in Operation Menace as part of the British force sent to the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dakar" title="Battle of Dakar">Battle of Dakar</a> in West Africa (23\xe2\x80\x9325 September 1940) in August 1940 to support an attempt by the <a href="/wiki/Free_French_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French Forces">Free French Forces</a> to overthrow the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy French</a> colonial government and install General <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>. Operation Menace failed, hampered by fog and misinformation about the extent of the town\'s defences, and the British forces withdrew on 26 September. Waugh\'s comment on the affair was this: \xe2\x80\xb3Bloodshed has been avoided at the cost of honour.\xe2\x80\xb3<sup id="cite_ref-Stannard16_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stannard16-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1940, Waugh was posted to a <a href="/wiki/Commando" title="Commando">commando</a> unit, and, after further training, became a member of "<a href="/wiki/Layforce" title="Layforce">Layforce</a>", under Colonel (later Brigadier) <a href="/wiki/Robert_Laycock" title="Robert Laycock">Robert Laycock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stannard16_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stannard16-119">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In February 1941, the unit sailed to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a>, where it participated in an unsuccessful attempt to recapture <a href="/wiki/Bardia" title="Bardia">Bardia</a>, on the Libyan coast.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In May, Layforce was required to assist in the evacuation of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>: Waugh was shocked by the disorder and its loss of discipline and, as he saw it, the cowardice of the departing troops.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> In July, during the roundabout journey home by troop ship, he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Put_Out_More_Flags" title="Put Out More Flags">Put Out More Flags</a></i> (1942), a novel of the war\'s early months in which he returned to the literary style he had used in the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Back in Britain, more training and waiting followed until, in May 1942, he was transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Horse_Guards" title="Royal Horse Guards">Royal Horse Guards</a>, on Laycock\'s recommendation.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> On 10 June 1942, Laura gave birth to Margaret, the couple\'s fourth child.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;n 5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Frustration.2C_Brideshead_and_Yugoslavia"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Frustration,_Brideshead_and_Yugoslavia">Frustration, <i>Brideshead</i> and Yugoslavia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Frustration, Brideshead and Yugoslavia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Waugh\'s elation at his transfer soon descended into disillusion as he failed to find opportunities for active service. The death of his father, on 26 June 1943, and the need to deal with family affairs prevented him from departing with his brigade for North Africa as part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Husky" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Husky">Operation Husky</a> (9 July\xe2\x80\x9317 August 1943), the Allied invasion of <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Despite his undoubted courage, his unmilitary and insubordinate character were rendering him effectively unemployable as a soldier.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> After spells of idleness at the regimental depot in <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Berkshire" title="Windsor, Berkshire">Windsor</a>, Waugh began parachute training at <a href="/wiki/Tatton_Park" title="Tatton Park">Tatton Park</a>, Cheshire, but landed awkwardly during an exercise and fractured a <a href="/wiki/Fibula" title="Fibula">fibula</a>. Recovering at Windsor, he applied for three months\' unpaid leave to write the novel that had been forming in his mind. His request was granted and, on 31 January 1944, he departed for <a href="/wiki/Chagford" title="Chagford">Chagford</a>, Devon, where he could work in seclusion. The result was <i><a href="/wiki/Brideshead_Revisited" title="Brideshead Revisited">Brideshead Revisited: The Sacred &amp; Profane Memories of Captain Charles Ryder</a></i> (1945),<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> the first of his explicitly Catholic novels of which the biographer <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Lane_Patey" title="Douglas Lane Patey">Douglas Lane Patey</a> commented that it was "the book that seemed to confirm his new sense of his writerly vocation".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh managed to extend his leave until June 1944. Soon after his return to duty he was recruited by <a href="/wiki/Randolph_Churchill" title="Randolph Churchill">Randolph Churchill</a> to serve in a military mission to <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>, and, early in July, flew with Churchill from <a href="/wiki/Bari" title="Bari">Bari</a>, Italy, to the Croatian island of <a href="/wiki/Vis_(island)" title="Vis (island)">Vis</a>. There, they met <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Marshal Tito</a>, the Communist leader of the <a href="/wiki/Partisans_(Yugoslavia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Partisans (Yugoslavia)">Partisans</a>, who was leading the guerrilla fight against the occupying <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis</a> forces with Allied support.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Waugh and Churchill returned to Bari before flying back to Yugoslavia to begin their mission, but their aeroplane crash-landed, both men were injured, and their mission was delayed for a month.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mission eventually arrived at <a href="/wiki/Topusko" title="Topusko">Topusko</a>, where it established itself in a deserted farmhouse. The group\'s liaison duties, between the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and the Communist Partisans, were light. Waugh had little sympathy with the Communist-led Partisans and despised Tito. His chief interest became the welfare of the Catholic Church in Croatia, which, he believed, had suffered at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Orthodox_Church" title="Serbian Orthodox Church">Serbian Orthodox Church</a> and would fare worse when the Communists took control.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> He expressed those thoughts in a long report, "Church and State in Liberated Croatia". After spells in <a href="/wiki/Dubrovnik" title="Dubrovnik">Dubrovnik</a> and Rome, Waugh returned to London on 15 March 1945 to present his report, which the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Office" title="Foreign and Commonwealth Office">Foreign Office</a> suppressed to maintain good relations with Tito, now the leader of communist Yugoslavia.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar">Postwar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Postwar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fame_and_fortune">Fame and fortune</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fame and fortune">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Brideshead Revisited</i> was published in London in May 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-Hastings494_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings494-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Waugh had been convinced of the book\'s qualities, "my first novel rather than my last".<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> It was a tremendous success, bringing its author fame, fortune and literary status.<sup id="cite_ref-Hastings494_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings494-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Happy though he was with this outcome, Waugh\'s principal concern as the war ended was the fate of the large populations of Eastern European Catholics, betrayed (as he saw it) into the hands of <a href="/wiki/Stalin" class="mw-redirect" title="Stalin">Stalin</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> by the Allies. He now saw little difference in morality between the war\'s combatants and later described it as "a sweaty tug-of-war between teams of indistinguishable louts".<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Although he took momentary pleasure from the defeat of <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1945" title="United Kingdom general election, 1945">1945 general election</a>, he saw the accession to power of the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> as a triumph of barbarism and the onset of a new "Dark Age".<sup id="cite_ref-Hastings494_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings494-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helena_of_Constantinople_(Cima_da_Conegliano).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Helena_of_Constantinople_%28Cima_da_Conegliano%29.jpg/170px-Helena_of_Constantinople_%28Cima_da_Conegliano%29.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Helena_of_Constantinople_%28Cima_da_Conegliano%29.jpg/255px-Helena_of_Constantinople_%28Cima_da_Conegliano%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Helena_of_Constantinople_%28Cima_da_Conegliano%29.jpg/340px-Helena_of_Constantinople_%28Cima_da_Conegliano%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5964" data-file-height="7437" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helena_of_Constantinople_(Cima_da_Conegliano).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Helena_(Empress)" class="mw-redirect" title="Helena (Empress)">St. Helena</a>, the subject of Waugh\'s 1950 novel</div></div></div>\n<p>In September 1945, after he was released by the army, he returned to Piers Court with his family (another daughter, Harriet, had been born at Pixton in 1944)<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> but spent much of the next seven years either in London, or travelling. In March 1946, he visited the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">Nuremberg trials</a>, and later that year, he was in Spain for a celebration of the 400th anniversary of the death of <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Vitoria" title="Francisco de Vitoria">Francisco de Vitoria</a>, said to be the founder of <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Waugh wrote up his experiences of the frustrations of postwar European travel in a novella, <i><a href="/wiki/Scott-King%27s_Modern_Europe" title="Scott-King&#39;s Modern Europe">Scott-King\'s Modern Europe</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey251_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey251-141">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In February 1947, he made the first of several trips to the United States, in the first instance to discuss filming of <i>Brideshead</i>. The project collapsed, but Waugh used his time in Hollywood to visit the <a href="/wiki/Forest_Lawn_Memorial_Park_(Hollywood_Hills)" title="Forest Lawn Memorial Park (Hollywood Hills)">Forest Lawn cemetery</a>, which provided the basis for his satire of American perspectives on death, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Loved_One" title="The Loved One">The Loved One</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In 1951 he visited the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> with his future biographer, Christopher Sykes,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> and in 1953, he travelled to <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a> to witness the final exhibition before burial of the remains of the 16th century Jesuit missionary-priest <a href="/wiki/Francis_Xavier" title="Francis Xavier">Francis Xavier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hastings554_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings554-143">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In between his journeys, Waugh worked intermittently on <i><a href="/wiki/Helena_(1950_novel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Helena (1950 novel)">Helena</a></i>, a long-planned novel about the discoverer of the <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">True Cross</a> that was "far the best book I have ever written or ever will write". Its success with the public was limited, but it was, his daughter Harriet later said, "the only one of his books that he ever cared to read aloud".<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1952 Waugh published <i><a href="/wiki/Men_at_Arms_(Evelyn_Waugh)" class="mw-redirect" title="Men at Arms (Evelyn Waugh)">Men at Arms</a></i>, the first of his semi-autobiographical war trilogy in which he depicted many of his personal experiences and encounters from the early stages of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Other books published during this period included <i><a href="/wiki/When_The_Going_Was_Good" class="mw-redirect" title="When The Going Was Good">When The Going Was Good</a></i> (1946),<sup id="cite_ref-Patey251_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey251-141">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> an anthology of his pre-war travel writing, <i>The Holy Places</i> (published by the <a href="/wiki/Ian_Fleming" title="Ian Fleming">Ian Fleming</a>-managed <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne_Press" title="Queen Anne Press">Queen Anne Press</a>, 1952) and <i><a href="/wiki/Love_Among_the_Ruins._A_Romance_of_the_Near_Future" title="Love Among the Ruins. A Romance of the Near Future">Love Among the Ruins</a></i> (1953), a <a href="/wiki/Dystopia" title="Dystopia">dystopian</a> tale in which Waugh displays his contempt for the modern world.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Nearing 50, Waugh was old for his years, "selectively deaf, rheumatic, irascible" and increasingly dependent on alcohol and on drugs to relieve his insomnia and depression.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Two more children, James (born 1946) and Septimus (born 1950), completed his family.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1945 onwards, Waugh became an avid collector of objects, particularly Victorian paintings and furniture. He filled Piers Court with his acquisitions, often from London\'s <a href="/wiki/Portobello_Market" class="mw-redirect" title="Portobello Market">Portobello Market</a> and from house clearance sales.<sup id="cite_ref-P153_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P153-149">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> His diary entry for 30 August 1946 records a visit to <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a>, where he bought "a lion of wood, finely carved for \xc2\xa325, also a bookcase \xc2\xa335... a charming Chinese painting \xc2\xa310, a Regency easel \xc2\xa37".<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Some of his buying was shrewd and prescient; he paid \xc2\xa310 for Rossetti\'s "Spirit of the Rainbow" to begin a collection of Victorian paintings that eventually acquired great value.
