Waugh also began, from 1949, to write knowledgeable reviews and articles on the subject of painting.<sup id="cite_ref-P153_149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P153-149">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;n 6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breakdown">Breakdown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Breakdown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By 1953, Waugh\'s popularity as a writer was declining. He was perceived as out of step with the <i><a href="/wiki/Zeitgeist" title="Zeitgeist">Zeitgeist</a></i>, and the large fees he demanded were no longer easily available.<sup id="cite_ref-Hastings554_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings554-143">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> His money was running out and progress on the second book of his war trilogy, <i><a href="/wiki/Officers_and_Gentlemen" title="Officers and Gentlemen">Officers and Gentlemen</a></i>, had stalled. Partly because of his dependency on drugs, his health was steadily deteriorating.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Shortage of cash led him to agree in November 1953 to be interviewed on BBC radio, where the panel took an aggressive line: "they tried to make a fool of me, and I don\'t think they entirely succeeded", Waugh wrote to Nancy Mitford.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Fleming_(writer)" title="Peter Fleming (writer)">Peter Fleming</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectator" title="The Spectator">The Spectator</a></i> likened the interview to "the goading of a bull by matadors".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early in 1954, Waugh\'s doctors, concerned by his physical deterioration, advised a change of scene. On 29 January, he took a ship bound for <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Ceylon</a>, hoping that he would be able to finish his novel. Within a few days, he was writing home complaining of "other passengers whispering about me" and of hearing voices, including that of his recent BBC <a href="/wiki/Interlocutor_(linguistics)" title="Interlocutor (linguistics)">interlocutor</a>, Stephen Black. He left the ship in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and flew on to <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a>, but, he wrote to Laura, the voices followed him.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Alarmed, Laura sought help from her friend, <a href="/wiki/Frances_Donaldson" class="mw-redirect" title="Frances Donaldson">Frances Donaldson</a>, whose husband agreed to fly out to Ceylon and bring Waugh home. In fact, Waugh made his own way back, now believing that he was being possessed by devils. A brief medical examination indicated that Waugh was suffering from <a href="/wiki/Bromism" title="Bromism">bromide poisoning</a> from his drugs regimen. When his medication was changed, the voices and the other hallucinations quickly disappeared.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Waugh was delighted, informing all of his friends that he had been mad: "Clean off my onion!". The experience was  fictionalised a few years later, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ordeal_of_Gilbert_Pinfold" title="The Ordeal of Gilbert Pinfold">The Ordeal of Gilbert Pinfold</a></i> (1957).<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1956, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Newman" title="Edwin Newman">Edwin Newman</a> made a short film about Waugh.  In the course of doing so, Newman learned that Waugh hated the modern world and wished that he had been born two or three centuries sooner.  Waugh disliked modern methods of transportation or communication, refusing to drive or use the telephone, and writing with a quill pen.  Waugh also expressed the view that American news reporters could not function without frequent infusions of <a href="/wiki/Whisky" title="Whisky">whisky</a> and that every American had been divorced at least once.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_works">Late works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Late works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Combe_Florey_-_near_the_Old_Manor_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_904793.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Combe_Florey_-_near_the_Old_Manor_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_904793.jpg/220px-Combe_Florey_-_near_the_Old_Manor_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_904793.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Combe_Florey_-_near_the_Old_Manor_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_904793.jpg/330px-Combe_Florey_-_near_the_Old_Manor_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_904793.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Combe_Florey_-_near_the_Old_Manor_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_904793.jpg/440px-Combe_Florey_-_near_the_Old_Manor_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_904793.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Combe_Florey_-_near_the_Old_Manor_House_-_geograph.org.uk_-_904793.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Combe_Florey" title="Combe Florey">Combe Florey</a>, the village in Somerset to which Waugh and his family moved in 1956</div></div></div>\n<p>Restored to health, Waugh returned to work and finished <i>Officers and Gentlemen</i>. In June 1955 the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Express" title="Daily Express">Daily Express</a></i> journalist and reviewer <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Spain" title="Nancy Spain">Nancy Spain</a>, accompanied by her friend Lord Noel-Buxton, arrived uninvited at Piers Court and demanded an interview. Waugh saw the pair off and wrote a wry account for <i>The Spectator</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> but he was troubled by the incident and decided to sell Piers Court: "I felt it was polluted", he told Nancy Mitford.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Late in 1956, the family moved to the manor house in the Somerset village of <a href="/wiki/Combe_Florey" title="Combe Florey">Combe Florey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> In January 1957, Waugh avenged the Spain\xe2\x80\x93Noel-Buxton intrusion by winning libel damages from the <i>Express</i> and Spain. The paper had printed an article by Spain that suggested that the sales of Waugh\'s books were much lower than they were and that his worth, as a journalist, was low.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Ordeal_of_Gilbert_Pinfold" title="The Ordeal of Gilbert Pinfold">Gilbert Pinfold</a></i> was published in the summer of 1957, "my barmy book", Waugh called it.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The extent to which the story is self-mockery, rather than true autobiography, became a subject of critical debate.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey339_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey339-164">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Waugh\'s next major book was a biography of his longtime friend <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Knox" title="Ronald Knox">Ronald Knox</a>, the Catholic writer and theologian who had died in August 1957. Research and writing extended over two years during which Waugh did little other work, delaying the third volume of his war trilogy. In June 1958, his son Auberon was severely wounded in a shooting accident while serving with the army in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>. Waugh remained detached; he neither went to Cyprus nor immediately visited Auberon on the latter\'s return to Britain. The critic and <a href="/wiki/Biography_in_literature" title="Biography in literature">literary biographer</a> David Wykes called Waugh\'s sang-froid "astonishing" and the family\'s apparent acceptance of his behaviour even more so.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although most of Waugh\'s books had sold well, and he had been well-rewarded for his journalism, his levels of expenditure meant that money problems and tax bills were a recurrent feature in his life.<sup id="cite_ref-H591_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H591-166">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> In 1950, as a means of <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">tax avoidance</a>, he had set up a trust fund for his children (he termed it the "Save the Children Fund", after the <a href="/wiki/Save_the_Children" title="Save the Children">well-established charity of that name</a>) into which he placed the initial advance and all future royalties from the Penguin (paperback) editions of his books.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> He was able to augment his personal finances by charging household items to the trust or selling his own possessions to it.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, by 1960, shortage of money led him to agree to an interview on BBC Television, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Face_to_Face_(British_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Face to Face (British TV series)">Face to Face</a></i> series conducted by <a href="/wiki/John_Freeman_(British_politician)" title="John Freeman (British politician)">John Freeman</a>. The interview was broadcast on 26 June 1960; according to his biographer <a href="/wiki/Selina_Hastings_(writer)" title="Selina Hastings (writer)">Selena Hastings</a>, Waugh restrained his instinctive hostility and coolly answered the questions put to him by Freeman, assuming what she describes as a "pose of world-weary boredom".<sup id="cite_ref-H591_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H591-166">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1960, Waugh was offered the honour of a <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_British_Empire" title="Order of the British Empire">CBE</a> but declined, believing that he should have been given the superior status of a <a href="/wiki/Knight_Bachelor" title="Knight Bachelor">knighthood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> In September, he produced his final travel book, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Tourist_in_Africa" title="A Tourist in Africa">A Tourist in Africa</a></i>, based on a visit made in January\xe2\x80\x93March 1959. He enjoyed the trip but "despised" the book. The critic <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Connolly" title="Cyril Connolly">Cyril Connolly</a> called it "the thinnest piece of book-making that Mr Waugh has undertaken".<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> The book done, he worked on the last of the war trilogy, which was published in 1961 as <i>Unconditional Surrender</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_death">Decline and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Decline and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Evelyn_Waugh_Grave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Evelyn_Waugh_Grave.jpg/170px-Evelyn_Waugh_Grave.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Evelyn_Waugh_Grave.jpg/255px-Evelyn_Waugh_Grave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Evelyn_Waugh_Grave.jpg/340px-Evelyn_Waugh_Grave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1140" data-file-height="1458" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Evelyn_Waugh_Grave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Waugh\'s grave in Combe Florey, adjacent to but not within the Anglican churchyard.</div></div></div>\n<p>As he approached his sixties, Waugh was in poor health, prematurely aged, "fat, deaf, short of breath", according to Patey.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> His biographer Martin Stannard likened his appearance around this time to that of "an exhausted rogue jollied up by drink".<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> In 1962 Waugh began work on his autobiography, and that same year wrote his final fiction, the long short story <i>Basil Seal Rides Again</i>. This revival of the protagonist of <i>Black Mischief</i> and <i>Put Out More Flags</i> was published in 1963; the <i><a href="/wiki/Times_Literary_Supplement" class="mw-redirect" title="Times Literary Supplement">Times Literary Supplement</a></i> called it a "nasty little book".<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> When the first volume of autobiography, <i>A Little Learning</i>, was published in 1964, Waugh\'s often oblique tone and discreet name changes ensured that friends avoided the embarrassments that some had feared.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh had welcomed the accession in 1958 of <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXIII" title="Pope John XXIII">Pope John XXIII</a><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> and wrote an appreciative tribute on the pope\'s death in 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> However, he became increasingly concerned by the decisions emerging from the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a>, which was convened by Pope John in October 1962 and continued under his successor, <a href="/wiki/Paul_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul VI">Paul VI</a> until 1965. Waugh, a staunch opponent of Church reform, was particularly distressed by the replacement of the universal <a href="/wiki/Latin_Mass" title="Latin Mass">Latin Mass</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In a <i>Spectator</i> article of 23 November 1962, he argued the case against change in a manner described by a later commentator as "sharp-edged reasonableness".<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> He wrote to Nancy Mitford that "the buggering up of the Church is a deep sorrow to me.... We write letters to the paper. A fat lot of good that does."<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1965, a new financial crisis arose from an apparent flaw in the terms of the "Save the Children" trust, and a large sum of back tax was being demanded. Waugh\'s agent, A.D. Peters, negotiated a settlement with the tax authorities for a manageable amount,<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> but in his concern to generate funds, Waugh signed contracts to write several books, including a history of the papacy, an illustrated book on the Crusades and a second volume of autobiography. Waugh\'s physical and mental deterioration prevented any work on these projects, and the contracts were cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> He described himself as "toothless, deaf, melancholic, shaky on my pins, unable to eat, full of dope, quite idle"<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> and expressed the belief that "all fates were worse than death".<sup id="cite_ref-Wykes209_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wykes209-184">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> His only significant literary activity in 1965 was the editing of the three war novels into a single volume, published as <i>Sword of Honour</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On Easter Day, 10 April 1966, after attending a Latin Mass in a neighbouring village with members of his family, Waugh died of heart failure at his Combe Florey home, at 62. He was buried, by special arrangement, in a consecrated plot outside the Anglican churchyard of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_St_Peter_%26_St_Paul,_Combe_Florey" title="Church of St Peter &amp; St Paul, Combe Florey">Church of St Peter &amp; St Paul, Combe Florey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Requiem_Mass" class="mw-redirect" title="Requiem Mass">Requiem Mass</a>, in Latin, was celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Cathedral" title="Westminster Cathedral">Westminster Cathedral</a> on 21 April 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Character_and_opinions">Character and opinions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Character and opinions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In the course of his lifetime, Waugh made enemies and offended many people; writer <a href="/wiki/James_Lees-Milne" title="James Lees-Milne">James Lees-Milne</a> said that Waugh "was the nastiest-tempered man in England".<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Waugh\'s son, <a href="/wiki/Auberon_Waugh" title="Auberon Waugh">Auberon</a>, said that the force of his father\'s personality was such that, despite his lack of height, "generals and chancellors of the exchequer, six-foot-six and exuding self-importance from every pore, quail[ed] in front of him".<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the biographic <i>Mad World</i> (2009), Paula Byrne said that the common view of Evelyn Waugh as a "snobbish misanthrope" is a caricature; he asks: "Why would a man, who was so unpleasant, be so beloved by such a wide circle of friends?"<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> His generosity to individual persons and causes, especially Catholic causes, extended to small gestures;<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> after his libel-court victory over <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Spain" title="Nancy Spain">Nancy Spain</a>, he sent her a bottle of champagne.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Hastings said that Waugh\'s outward personal belligerence to strangers was not entirely serious but an attempt at "finding a sparring partner worthy of his own wit and ingenuity".<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Besides mocking others, Waugh mocked himself\xe2\x80\x94the elderly buffer, "crusty colonel" image, which he presented in later life, was a comic impersonation, and not his true self.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As an instinctive conservative, Waugh believed that class divisions, with inequalities of wealth and position, were natural and that "no form of government [was] ordained by God as being better than any other".<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> In the post-war "Age of the Common Man", he attacked socialism (the "Cripps\xe2\x80\x93Attlee terror")<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> and complained, after Churchill\'s election in 1951, that "the Conservative Party have never put the clock back a single second".<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Waugh never voted in elections; in 1959, he expressed a hope that the Conservatives would win the election, which they did, but would not vote for them, saying "I should feel I was morally inculpated in their follies" and added: "I do not aspire to advise my sovereign in her choice of servants".<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh\'s Catholicism was fundamental: "The Church&#160;... is the normal state of man from which men have disastrously exiled themselves."<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> He believed that the Catholic Church was the last, great defence against the encroachment of the Dark Age being ushered in by the <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> and the spreading of <a href="/wiki/Working_class_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Working class culture">working class culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-H503_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H503-201">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Strictly observant, Waugh admitted to Diana Cooper that his most difficult task was how to square the obligations of his faith with his indifference to his fellow men.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> When Nancy Mitford asked him how he reconciled his often objectionable conduct with being a Christian, Waugh replied that "were he not a Christian he would be even more horrible".<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh\'s conservatism was <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetic</a> as well as political and religious.  Although he praised younger writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Angus_Wilson" title="Angus Wilson">Angus Wilson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muriel_Spark" title="Muriel Spark">Muriel Spark</a> and <a href="/wiki/V.S._Naipaul" class="mw-redirect" title="V.S. Naipaul">V.S. Naipaul</a>, he was scornful of the 1950s writers\' group known as "<a href="/wiki/The_Movement_(literature)" title="The Movement (literature)">The Movement</a>". He said that the literary world was "sinking into black disaster" and that literature might die within thirty years.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> As a schoolboy, Waugh had praised the art of <a href="/wiki/Cubism" title="Cubism">Cubism</a> but soon abandoned his interest in artistic <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">Modernism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> In 1945, Waugh said that <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a>\'s artistic standing was the result of a "mesmeric trick" and that his paintings "could not be intelligently discussed in the terms used of the civilised <a href="/wiki/Old_Masters" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Masters">masters</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> In 1953, in a radio interview, he named <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Egg" title="Augustus Egg">Augustus Egg</a> (1816\xe2\x80\x931863) as a painter for whom he had particular esteem.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;n 7&#93;</a></sup> Despite their political differences, Waugh came to admire <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>, because of their shared patriotism and sense of <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout his literary works, Evelyn Waugh freely expressed <a href="/wiki/Racialism" title="Racialism">racial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Semitism">anti-semitic</a> prejudices, especially in the books he wrote before the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>. The writer <a href="/wiki/V.S._Pritchett" class="mw-redirect" title="V.S. Pritchett">V.S. Pritchett</a> said that Waugh\'s anti-semitism, "like <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a>, is there, nonviolent, but undeniable". Wykes said that anti-semitism is Waugh\'s "most persistent nastiness",<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> adding that Waugh\'s racism was "an illogical extension of his views on the naturalness and rightness of <a href="/wiki/Hierarchy" title="Hierarchy">hierarchy</a> as the [main] principle of social organisation".<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> As an admirer of Waugh\'s writing, Orwell said that Evelyn Waugh was "almost as good a novelist as it is possible to be... while holding untenable opinions".<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes_and_style">Themes and style</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Themes and style">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Wykes observes that Waugh\'s novels reprise and fictionalise the principal events of his life, although in an early essay Waugh wrote: "Nothing is more insulting to a novelist than to assume that he is incapable of anything but the mere transcription of what he observes".<sup id="cite_ref-Wykes209_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wykes209-184">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> The reader should not assume that the author agreed with the opinions expressed by his fictional characters.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, in the Introduction to the <i>Complete Short Stories</i>, Ann Pasternak Slater said that the "delineation of social prejudices and the language in which they are expressed is part of Waugh\'s meticulous observation of his contemporary world".<sup id="cite_ref-Slater_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slater-213">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The critic <a href="/wiki/Clive_James" title="Clive James">Clive James</a> said of Waugh: "Nobody ever wrote a more unaffectedly elegant English... its hundreds of years of steady development culminate in him".<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> As his talent developed and matured, he maintained what literary critic Andrew Michael Roberts called "an exquisite sense of the ludicrous, and a fine aptitude for exposing false attitudes".<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> In the first stages of his 40-year writing career, before his conversion to Catholicism in 1930, Waugh was the novelist of the <a href="/wiki/Bright_Young_People" class="mw-redirect" title="Bright Young People">Bright Young People</a> generation. His first two novels, <i><a href="/wiki/Decline_and_Fall" title="Decline and Fall">Decline and Fall</a></i> (1928) and <i><a href="/wiki/Vile_Bodies" title="Vile Bodies">Vile Bodies</a></i> (1930), comically reflect a futile society, populated by two-dimensional, basically unbelievable characters in circumstances too fantastic to evoke the reader\'s emotions.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollis5_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollis5-216">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> A typical Waugh trademark evident in the early novels is rapid, unattributed dialogue in which the participants can be readily identified.<sup id="cite_ref-Slater_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slater-213">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> At the same time Waugh was writing serious essays, such as "The War and the Younger Generation" in which he castigates his own generation as "crazy and sterile" people.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh\'s conversion to Catholicism did not noticeably change the nature of his next two novels, <i><a href="/wiki/Black_Mischief" title="Black Mischief">Black Mischief</a></i> (1934) and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Handful_of_Dust" title="A Handful of Dust">A Handful of Dust</a></i> (1934), but, in the latter novel, the elements of <a href="/wiki/Farce" title="Farce">farce</a> are subdued, and the protagonist, Tony Last, is recognisably a person rather than a comic cipher.<sup id="cite_ref-Hollis5_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hollis5-216">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Waugh\'s first fiction with a Catholic theme was the short story "Out of Depth" (1933) about the immutability of the Mass.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> From the mid-1930s onwards, Catholicism and conservative politics were much featured in his journalistic and non-fiction writing<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> before he reverted to his former manner with <i>Scoop</i> (1938), a novel about journalism, journalists, and unsavoury journalistic practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey157_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey157-220">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <i>Work Suspended and Other Stories</i>  Waugh introduced "real" characters and a first-person narrator, signalling the literary style he would adopt in <i>Brideshead Revisited</i> a few years later.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>  <i>Brideshead</i>, which questions the meaning of human existence without God, is the first novel in which Evelyn Waugh clearly presents his conservative religious and political views.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In the <i>LIFE</i> magazine article, "Fan Fare" (1946), Waugh said that "you can only leave God out [of fiction] by making your characters pure abstractions" and that his future novels shall be "the attempt to represent man more fully which, to me, means only one thing, man in his relation to God."<sup id="cite_ref-FF_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FF-222">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> As such, the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Helena_(1950_novel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Helena (1950 novel)">Helena</a></i> (1950) is Evelyn Waugh\'s most philosophically Christian book.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <i>Brideshead</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Proletarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Proletarian">proletarian</a> junior officer Hooper illustrates a theme that persists in Waugh\'s postwar fiction: the rise of mediocrity in the "Age of the Common Man".<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In the trilogy <i><a href="/wiki/Sword_of_Honour" title="Sword of Honour">Sword of Honour</a></i>  (<i>Men at Arms</i>, 1952; <i>Officers and Gentlemen</i>, 1955, <i>Unconditional Surrender</i>, 1961) the social pervasiveness of mediocrity is personified in the semi-comical character "Trimmer", a sloven and a fraud who triumphs by contrivance.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> In the novella "<a href="/wiki/Scott-King%27s_Modern_Europe" title="Scott-King&#39;s Modern Europe">Scott-King\'s Modern Europe</a>" (1947), Waugh\'s pessimism about the future is in the schoolmaster\'s admonition: "I think it would be very wicked, indeed, to do anything to fit a boy for the modern world".<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Likewise, such cynicism pervades the novel <i>Love Among the Ruins</i> (1953), set in a dystopian, welfare-state Britain that is so socially disagreeable that <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a> is the most sought-after of the government\'s social services.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Of the postwar novels, Patey says that  <i>The Ordeal of Gilbert Pinfold</i> (1957) stands out \xe2\x80\xb3a kind of mock-novel, a sly invitation to a game\xe2\x80\xb3.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey339_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey339-164">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Waugh\'s final work of fiction, "Basil Seal Rides Again" (1962), features characters from the prewar novels; Waugh admitted that the work was a \xe2\x80\xb3senile attempt to recapture the manner of my youth\xe2\x80\xb3.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Stylistically this final story begins in the same fashion as the first story, \xe2\x80\xb3The Balance" of 1926, with a "fusillade of unattributed dialogue".<sup id="cite_ref-Slater_213-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slater-213">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Of Waugh\'s early books, <i>Decline and Fall</i> was hailed by <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Bennett" title="Arnold Bennett">Arnold Bennett</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Evening_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Evening Standard">Evening Standard</a></i> as "an uncompromising and brilliantly malicious satire".<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> The critical reception of <i>Vile Bodies</i> two years later was even more enthusiastic, with Rebecca West predicting that Waugh was "destined to be the dazzling figure of his age".<sup id="cite_ref-Patey33_76-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey33-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> However, <i>A Handful of Dust</i>, later widely regarded as a masterpiece, received a more muted welcome from critics, despite the author\'s own high estimation of the work.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> The book\'s ending, with Tony Last condemned forever to read Dickens to his mad jungle captor, was thought by the critic <a href="/wiki/Henry_Green" title="Henry Green">Henry Yorke</a> to reduce an otherwise believable book to "phantasy". <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Connolly" title="Cyril Connolly">Cyril Connolly</a>\'s first reaction to the book was that Waugh\'s powers were failing, an opinion that he later revised.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the latter 1930s, Waugh\'s inclination to Catholic and conservative polemics affected his standing with the general reading public.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Campion biography is said by David Wykes to be "so rigidly biased that it has no claims to make as history".<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> The pro-fascist tone in parts of <i>Waugh in Abyssinia</i> offended readers and critics and prevented its publication in America.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> There was general relief among critics when <i>Scoop</i>, in 1938, indicated a return to Waugh\'s earlier comic style. Critics had begun to think that his wit had been displaced by partisanship and propaganda.<sup id="cite_ref-Patey157_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Patey157-220">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Waugh maintained his reputation in 1942, with <i>Put Out More Flags</i>, which sold well despite wartime restrictions on paper and printing.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> Its public reception, however, did not compare with that accorded to <i>Brideshead Revisited</i> three years later, on both sides of the Atlantic. <i>Brideshead\'</i>s selection as the American <a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Month_Club" title="Book of the Month Club">Book of the Month</a> swelled its US sales to an extent that dwarfed those in Britain, which was affected by paper shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Despite the public\'s enthusiasm, critical opinion was split. <i>Brideshead\'s</i> Catholic standpoint offended some critics who had greeted Waugh\'s earlier novels with warm praise.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Its perceived snobbery and its deference to the aristocracy were attacked by, among others, <a href="/wiki/Conor_Cruise_O%27Brien" title="Conor Cruise O&#39;Brien">Conor Cruise O\'Brien</a> who, in the Irish literary magazine <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bell_(magazine)" title="The Bell (magazine)">The Bell</a></i>, wrote of Waugh\'s "almost mystical veneration" for the upper classes.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Fellow writer <a href="/wiki/Rose_Macaulay" title="Rose Macaulay">Rose Macaulay</a> believed that Waugh\'s genius had been adversely affected by the intrusion of his right-wing partisan <i>alter ego</i> and that he had lost his detachment: "In art so naturally ironic and detached as his, this is a serious loss".<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, the book was praised by Yorke, <a href="/wiki/Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene">Graham Greene</a> and, in glowing terms, by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Acton" title="Harold Acton">Harold Acton</a> who was particularly impressed by its evocation of 1920s Oxford.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> In 1959, at the request of publishers Chapman and Hall and in some deference to his critics, Waugh revised the book and wrote in a preface: "I have modified the grosser passages but not obliterated them because they are an essential part of the book".<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In "Fan Fare", Waugh forecasts that his future books will be unpopular because of their religious theme.<sup id="cite_ref-FF_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FF-222">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> On publication in 1950, <i>Helena</i> was received indifferently by the public and by critics, who disparaged the awkward mixing of 20th century schoolgirl slang with otherwise reverential prose.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> Otherwise, Waugh\'s prediction proved unfounded; all his fiction remained in print and sales stayed healthy. During his successful 1957 lawsuit against the <i>Daily Express</i>, Waugh\'s counsel produced figures showing total sales to that time of over four million books, two thirds in Britain and the rest in America.<sup id="cite_ref-S382_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S382-243">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> <i>Men at Arms</i>, the first volume of his war trilogy, won the <a href="/wiki/James_Tait_Black_Memorial_Prize" title="James Tait Black Memorial Prize">James Tait Black Memorial Prize</a> in 1953;<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> initial critical comment was lukewarm, with Connolly likening <i>Men at Arms</i> to beer rather than champagne.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Connolly changed his view later, calling the completed trilogy "the finest novel to come out of the war".<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Of Waugh\'s other major postwar works, the Knox biography was admired within Waugh\'s close circle but criticised by others in the Church for its depiction of Knox as an unappreciated victim of the Catholic hierarchy.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> The book did not sell well\xe2\x80\x94"like warm cakes", according to Waugh.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> <i>Pinfold</i> surprised the critics by its originality. Its plainly autobiographical content, Hastings suggests, gave the public a fixed image of Waugh: "stout, splenetic, red-faced and reactionary, a figure from burlesque complete with cigar, bowler hat and loud checked suit."<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reputation">Reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Reputation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1973, Waugh\'s diaries were serialised in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> prior to publication in book form in 1976. The revelations about his private life, thoughts and attitudes created controversy. Although Waugh had removed embarrassing entries relating to his Oxford years and his first marriage, there was sufficient left on the record to enable enemies to project a negative image of the writer as intolerant, snobbish and sadistic, with pronounced fascist leanings.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Some of this picture, it was maintained by Waugh\'s supporters, arose from poor editing of the diaries, and a desire to transform Waugh from a writer to a "character".<sup id="cite_ref-Wheatcroft_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheatcroft-250">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, a popular conception developed of Waugh as a monster.<sup id="cite_ref-Hastings627_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings627-251">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> When, in 1980, a selection of his letters was published, his reputation became the subject of further discussion. <a href="/wiki/Philip_Larkin" title="Philip Larkin">Philip Larkin</a>, reviewing the collection in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, thought that it demonstrated Waugh\'s elitism; to receive a letter from him, it seemed, "one would have to have a nursery nickname and be a member of <a href="/wiki/White%27s" title="White&#39;s">White\'s</a>, a Roman Catholic, a high-born lady or an Old Etonian novelist".<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_Howard,_Yorkshire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Castle_Howard%2C_Yorkshire.jpg/220px-Castle_Howard%2C_Yorkshire.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Castle_Howard%2C_Yorkshire.jpg/330px-Castle_Howard%2C_Yorkshire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Castle_Howard%2C_Yorkshire.jpg/440px-Castle_Howard%2C_Yorkshire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castle_Howard,_Yorkshire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Castle_Howard" title="Castle Howard">Castle Howard</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>, was used to represent "Brideshead" in the 1982 television series and in a subsequent 2008 film.</div></div></div>\n<p>The publication of the diaries and letters promoted increased interest in Waugh and his works and caused publication of much new material. Christopher Sykes\'s biography had appeared in 1975, between 1980 and 1998 three more full biographies were issued and other biographical and critical studies have continued to be produced. A collection of Waugh\'s journalism and reviews was published in 1983, revealing a fuller range of his ideas and beliefs. The new material provided further grounds for debate between Waugh\'s supporters and detractors.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The 1982 <a href="/wiki/Granada_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Granada Television">Granada Television</a> <a href="/wiki/Brideshead_Revisited_(TV_serial)" title="Brideshead Revisited (TV serial)">adaptation of <i>Brideshead Revisited</i></a> introduced a new generation to Waugh\'s works, in Britain and in America.<sup id="cite_ref-Hastings627_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings627-251">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> There had been earlier television treatment of Waugh\'s fiction, as <i>Sword of Honour</i> had been serialised by the <a href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="British Broadcasting Corporation">BBC</a> in 1967, but the impact of Granada\'s <i>Brideshead</i> was much wider. Its nostalgic depiction of a vanished form of Englishness appealed to the American mass market;<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine\'s TV critic described the series as "a novel... made into a poem", and listed it among the "100 Best TV Shows of All Time".<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> There have been further cinematic Waugh adaptations: <i>A Handful of Dust</i> in 1988, <i>Vile Bodies</i> (filmed as <i>Bright Young Things</i>) in 2003 and <i>Brideshead</i> again in 2008. These popular treatments have maintained the public\'s appetite for Waugh\'s novels, all of which remain in print and continue to sell.<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Several have been listed among various  compiled lists of the world\'s greatest novels.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;n 8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Stannard concludes that beneath his public mask, Waugh was "a dedicated artist and a man of earnest faith, struggling against the dryness of his soul".<sup id="cite_ref-StannardODNB_26-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StannardODNB-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene">Graham Greene</a>, in a letter to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> shortly after Waugh\'s death, acknowledged him as "the greatest novelist of my generation",<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine\'s obituarist called him "the grand old mandarin of modern British prose" and asserted that his novels "will continue to survive as long as there are readers who can savor what critic <a href="/wiki/V._S._Pritchett" title="V. S. Pritchett">V. S. Pritchett</a> calls \'the beauty of his malice\'&#160;".<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> Nancy Mitford said of him in a television interview, "What nobody remembers about Evelyn is that everything with him was jokes. Everything. That\'s what none of the people who wrote about him seem to have taken into account at all".<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For a listing of Waugh\'s works, see <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Waugh_bibliography" title="Evelyn Waugh bibliography">Evelyn Waugh bibliography</a>.</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some biographers have recorded his forenames as "Evelyn Arthur St. John", but Waugh gives the "Arthur Evelyn" order in <i>A Little Learning</i>, p. 27. The confusion may in part be attributable to differences in the forename order between Waugh\'s birth and death certificates. The former specifies "Arthur Evelyn St. John," and the latter "Evelyn Arthur St. John."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1993 a <a href="/wiki/Blue_plaque" title="Blue plaque">blue plaque</a> commemorating Waugh\'s residence was installed at Underhill, which by then had become 145 North End Road, Golders Green.<sup id="cite_ref-EngHet_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EngHet-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A biography of Roxburgh (who went on to be first headmaster of <a href="/wiki/Stowe_School" title="Stowe School">Stowe School</a>) was the last work given a literary review by Waugh, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> on 17 October 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Cruttwell" is a brutal burglar in <i>Decline and Fall</i>, a snobbish Member of Parliament in <i>Vile Bodies</i>, a social parasite in <i>Black Mischief</i>, a disreputable osteopath in <i>A Handful of Dust</i> and a salesman with a fake tan in <i>Scoop</i>. The homicidal Loveday in "Mr. Loveday\'s Little Outing" was originally "Mr. Cruttwell". See Hastings, pp. 173, 209, 373; Stannard, Vol. I pp. 342, 389</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Earlier, Laura had borne a daughter, christened Mary, on 1 December 1940, but she lived only a few hours.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See, for example, "Rossetti Revisited", 1949 (Gallagher (ed.), pp. 377\xe2\x80\x9379; "Age of Unrest", 1954 (Gallagher (ed.), pp. 459\xe2\x80\x9360; "The Death of Painting", 1956 (Gallagher (ed.) pp. 503\xe2\x80\x9307</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Excerpts from the text of the broadcast, on 16 November 1953, are given in the 1998 Penguin Books edition of <i>The Ordeal of Gilbert Pinfold</i>, p. 135\xe2\x80\x93143</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-257"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-257">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <i>Time</i> magazine\'s "All Time 100 Novels";<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Observer</i> critics\' "100 greatest novels of all time";<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Random House Modern Library\'s "100 Best Novels".<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Evelyn_Waugh&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em;
